SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
3 Subdrainage
Seepage conditions may be present at localized areas along the proposed roadway
alignments as evident in some areas noted during our site reconnaissance
Therefore we recommend that provisions be incorporated into the construction
documents to provide for subdrains in the following areas
at all springs and seepage areas
_ at the bottom of canyon fills
on the uphill side of keyways and
in other areas of the site where seepage is observed during and after
grading or as recommended by the Engineer during construction
At stream crossings and other spring seepage areas where drainage structures
such as reinforced box and pipe culverts are planned it is our opinion that a
subdrain system for subterranean seepage would not be necessary We believe
that the bedding material underneath the drainage structures may serve as a
subdrain system in these areas
Where needed subdrains should consist of perforated pipes with perforations
placed facing down and should be at least 100 mm in diameter All subdrains
should be surrounded and underlain by at least 100 mm of drain rock
A non woven geotextile such as Mirafi 180N or equivalent should wrap around the
drain rock In general subdrain trenches should be at least 300 mm wide and at
least 600 mm deep A sketch of the Typical Subdrain Details is presented on
Plate 6 Subdrains should be daylighted into drainage ditches culverts or other
drainage structures for proper discharge
4 Site Preparation
It should be noted that the existing terrain along the majority of the Palau Compact
Road alignments is heavily vegetated Therefore substantial site preparation work
involving clearing and grubbing would be required for the subject project At the
on set of earthwork areas within the contract grading limits should be thoroughly
cleared and grubbed In areas where mangrove plants are present the existing
W O 3595 00 B TO Bagge 44
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
ground should be cleared of the surface vegetation prior to the placement of
geogrids and a stabilization layer Grubbing of the mangrove areas would be
difficult and will not be required All vegetation debris rubbish and other
unsuitable materials should be removed and disposed of properly beyond the limits
of grading to reduce the potential for contamination of the excavated materials
Soft and yielding areas including undocumented fill areas encountered during
clearing and grubbing below any areas designated to receive fill not designated as
soft soil areas should be over excavated to expose firm natural material The
resulting excavation should be backfilled with well compacted general fill The
excavated soft soils should be properly disposed of beyond the limits of grading
Based on our field exploration results significant soft soil deposits should be
expected along portions of the roadway alignments We understand that complete
removal and replacement of soft soil deposits may not be feasible in these areas
due to the significant depth and areal extent of the soft soil deposits Therefore
these areas should be stabilized in general accordance with the recommendations
presented in the Soft Ground Stabilization section of this report prior to fill
placement It should be noted that where soft ground conditions are present a
settlement waiting period and settlement monitoring would be required
Based on our field observations undocumented fills were placed for the
maintenance of the existing Babeldaob Road which intersects portions of the
proposed Palau Compact Road alignment These undocumented fills may be
deposited within the proposed Package B alignment as potentially loose or soft fill
slopes bordering the existing road These undocumented fills where encountered
within the grading limits of the proposed roadway alignment should be completely
removed to expose firm materials and recompacted to general fill requirements
In general the over excavated subgrades and areas designated to receive fills
should be proof rolled to a minimum of 85 percent relative compaction Relative
compaction refers to the in place dry density of soil expressed as a percentage of
W O 3595 00 B Page 45
GEOLABS HAWAIH
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
the maximum dry density of the same soil established in accordance with
ASTM Test Designation D T 180 using the moist preparation
method wet to dry Optimum moisture is the water content corresponding to the
maximum dry density Saturation and subsequent yielding of the exposed
subgrade due to inclement weather and poor drainage may require over excavation
of the soft areas and replacement with compacted general fill
Our field exploration indicated that the in situ soils along the proposed roadway
alignments generally exist in a very moist to wet condition Therefore it would be
very difficult to achieve the normal 90 percent relative compaction requirement
without significant aeration of the in situ soils Since the project site is located in an
area that is subjected to high rainfall throughout the year substantial aeration of the
in situ soils may not be feasible and practical during the project construction As a
result we believe that the compaction requirement will need to be lowered to a
minimum of 85 percent relative compaction for subgrade preparation and fill
placement operations Use of the vibratory action of a compactor should be
carefully controlled to avoid inducing a pumping subgrade condition Detailed
discussions are presented in the Fill Placement and Compaction Requirements
subsection of this report
If the subgrade soils are pumping or yielding during subgrade preparation the
subgrade should be stabilized prior to fill placement and or pavement construction
Subgrade stabilization measures may include the use of additives such as cement
or lime treatment and or the incorporation of ground stabilization fabrics Detailed
discussions are presented in the Subgrade Stabilization section of this report
5 Fills and Backfills
In general we believe that the excavated materials may be used as a source of
general fill for embankment construction provided that the materials are properly
processed and placed in accordance with the recommendations presented in the
Fill Placement and Compaction Requirements subsection of this report
Soft and or loose materials generated from excavations into the recent alluvial and
W O 3595 00 B Page 46
GEOLABS HAWAI
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
mangrove swamp deposits should not be used as fills and should be disposed of
properly beyond the limits of grading
Granular fills will be required for construction of the causeway embankment and in
other designated areas Granular fill should be well graded from coarse to fine with
no particles larger than 150 mm in largest dimension and should contain not more
than 30 percent particles less than The material should have a
laboratory CBR value of 20 or more and should have a maximum swell value of
1 percent or less We believe that granular materials obtained from dredging in
parts of Palau may be used as a potential source of granular fill provided that the
material is free of clay lumps or balls processed to meet the project specifications
and compacted to the specified requirements
Surge rock fill should consist of clean gravel crushed stone rock or coral and
should be free from organic vegetable matter or other deleterious substances
The material should be free draining graded and range in size from 30 mm to
300 mm
Drain rock should consist of clean gravel crushed stone rock or coral and
should be free from organic vegetable matter or other deleterious substances
The material should be free draining and should range in size from 10 mm to
40 mm
Fill and backfill materials within the upper 1 meter from the finished subgrades
should consist of materials less than 150 mm in largest dimension Materials
generated from excavations into the dense volcanic breccia and hard tuff rock
formations may be used as a source of fill and backfill materials within the upper
1 meter from the finished subgrades provided that they are screened of over sized
materials greater than 150 mm in largest dimension and processed to provide a
relatively well graded material to prevent the occurrence of voids in the compacted
mass Fill materials placed within future utility corridors should also be less than
150 mm in maximum dimension to facilitate future utility trench excavations
W O 3595 00 B Page 47
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
Over sized rock fragments may be disposed of off site used in deep fills or broken
down to smaller sized materials and incorporated into the fill materials If the
excavated rock materials do not contain sufficient smaller sized materials to
produce a well graded material off site borrow or on site rock crushing of
large sized rock fragments or boulders should be considered to provide the
required gradation and particle size to develop a well graded material In general
placement of boulders in fills should be in accordance with the procedures
recommended in the Boulder Disposal subsection of this report
6 Fill Placement and Compaction Requirements
Since the project site is located in a high rainfall area and the on site materials
generally have high in situ moisture contents ranging from about 50 to over
80 percent which is approximately 20 to 50 percent above the optimum moisture
content significant drying of the excavated materials will be required prior to their
use as fill and or backfill material in order to meet the normal 90 percent minimum
compaction requirement
As mentioned earlier substantial aeration of the in situ soils may not be feasible
due to the high rainfall environment at the project site Based on past project
experience in Palau and our experience with similar high moisture tropical soils we
believe that the compaction requirement for general fill placement for this project
may be reduced from the standard of 90 percent to a minimum of 85 percent
relative compaction Relative compaction refers to the in place dry density of soil
expressed as a percentage of the maximum dry density of the same soil
established in accordance with ASTM _ Test Designation D 1557 91
AASHTO T 180 using the moist preparation method wet to dry Optimum
moisture is the water content corresponding to the maximum dry density
The reduction in compaction criteria will result in a reduction in the shear strength of
the fill materials however we believe that sufficient strength can be obtained with
the lower compaction criteria for the proposed embankments However settlement
of the fills due to self weight especially in deep fills areas should be expected and
is further discussed in the Ground Settlement section of this report
W O 3595 00 B Page 48
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
Fills and backfills up to the finished subgrade of the pavement should be
moisture conditioned to above the optimum moisture content placed in level lifts on
the order of about 150 to 200 mm in loose thickness and compacted to at least
85 percent relative compaction Even with the reduced 85 percent minimum
compaction criteria we believe that some moisture reduction would still be
necessary especially for materials generated from the deep excavations where the
in situ moisture contents may be in excess of 65 to 70 percent
The Contractor working on this project should be made aware that aeration to lower
the soil moisture and more compaction effort of thinner fill lifts to achieve the
specified compaction will generally reduce the rate of fill placement for this project
Compaction should be accomplished by sheepsfoot rollers vibratory rollers or
other types of acceptable compaction equipment Water tamping jetting or
ponding should not be allowed to compact the on site silty and clayey soils Due to
the high rainfall environment at the project site we believe that good construction
practices such as sealing and grading of the ground surface to provide positive
drainage at the end of each work day to reduce the potential for ponding and
saturation are essential to the earthwork operations for the proposed roadway
project
Where granular fills are used for earthwork construction such as for the causeway
fills and other areas we recommend that the compaction requirement for granular
fill materials be increased to a minimum of 90 percent relative compaction
In general granular fills should be moisture conditioned to above the optimum
moisture content placed in level loose lifts not exceeding about 200 mm and
compacted to not less than 90 percent relative compaction
Preparation and compaction of the pavement and shoulder subgrades and
placement and compaction of the subbase course aggregate base course cement
treated subbase and cement treated base are further discussed in the Subgrade
Subbase and Base Preparation section of this report
W O 3595 00 B Page 49
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS

B Reinforced Earth Slopes
As discussed earlier under the Fill Slope Design subsection of this report a fill
slope inclination steeper than 2H 1V may be required in localized areas along the
proposed roadway alignment due to site specific conditions In general we believe that fill
slopes may be designed with a slope inclination as steep as 1H 1V provided that the earth
materials are reinforced with adequate reinforced slope geogrids or geotextiles to
strengthen the fill soils
Reinforced slope geogrids are generally polymer grid structures with high tensile
strength It generally provides a cost effective solution to slope stability problems which
may include the following insufficient right of way high surcharge loads poor quality fills
high seismic forces steep slopes or difficult landslide repairs When reinforced slope
geogrids are placed in soil the grid geometry interlocks with the adjacent soil creating a
soil geogrid composite with greatly enhanced engineering properties Different grid
configurations are available to provide optimum soil grid interaction for a range of soil
types and slope reinforcement applications Reinforced slope geotextiles work in a similar
manner to reinforced slope geogrids The construction methodology is briefly described
as follows
As the slope is constructed horizontal layers of reinforced slope geogrids or
geotextiles are placed in the compacted fill at predetermined levels The lengths of the
reinforced slope geogrid or geotextile layers are designed to anchor potential failure zones
into stable interior sections of the embankment or hillside As forces develop within a soil
mass the high modulus reinforced slope geogrids or geotextiles are immediately pulled
into tension The reinforced slope geogrids or geotextiles transfer this tensile force from
the unstable soil back into the less stressed portions of the slope and stability is thus
maintained
Based on the laboratory tests performed on the remolded samples of the primary fill
material general fill we believe that a friction angle on the order of 28 to 30 degrees and
a wet density of about m may be used for the preliminary design analyses of the
reinforced earth slopes A friction angle of 30 degrees and a wet density of m
W O 3595 00 B Page 53
GEOLABS HAWAI
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
may be used for the foundation soils We recommend that a minimum of 2 meters of
unreinforced soils below the pavement surface elevation be provided to reduce the
potential for encountering the geogrids or geotextiles during the installation of the guard
rails or poles for signs etc The upper portion of the unreinforced fill slope may be sloped
at an inclination of 2H 1V It should be noted that a cost benefit analysis should be
conducted of reinforced earth slopes to evaluate if reinforced earth slopes are
economically viable for each area
C Subgrade Stabilization
The on site soils along the majority of the Palau Compact Road alignment generally
exist in a very moist to wet condition Based on our laboratory tests the recompacted
saprolite soils exhibit relatively low California Bearing Ratio CBR values of about 1 to 5
Strengthening of these subgrade soils may be required in certain areas in order to improve
the subsoil workability and to facilitate the project construction This may be accomplished
by the inclusion of additives such as lime or cement treatment and or incorporation of
ground stabilization fabrics at the subgrade level
1 Lime and or Cement Stabilization
Lime has been used to improve the properties of fine grained soils for many years
The general objectives of lime stabilization are to modify and improve the
characteristics of the soils thus expediting construction and improving the strength
and durability of the soils In particular it is desired to control the volume stability
swelling and shrinkage characteristics and to reduce the plasticity of the soils
When lime is added to clayey soils several chemical reactions are initiated These
reactions are cation exchange and flocculation agglomeration they are also
pozzolanic In the cation and flocculation agglomeration reactions the clay
particles tend to bond together to form larger sized particles These reactions tend
to decrease the liquid limit increase the plastic limit decrease the plasticity index
increase the workability and improve the strength and deformation properties of the
clay
W O 3595 00 B Page 54
GEOLABS HAWAI
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
The pozzolanic reactions involve the formation of cementitious materials between
lime and the silica and alumina of the clay The end product is a series of calcium
silicate hydrates It is important that a sufficient amount of lime be present to
provide a high environment In the long term the high pH causes a
breakdown of the crystal lattice of the clay and the formation of cementitious
products These reactions can continue for a long period of time
It should be noted that the presence of free sulfates in clay soils can be detrimental
With high sulfate content in the soil the initial stabilization may be satisfactory but
subsequent wetting can lead to expansion and breakdown of the cement structure
Attainment of a uniform and intimate mixture of lime with clay and proper curing in
the field are of vital importance and affect the quality of the end results
It is not anticipated that widespread use of lime for subgrade soil stabilization would
be implemented for this project especially since the quick lime needed for soil
stabilization is not readily available in the Republic of Palau and would need to be
specifically imported for this application However cement treatment may be
considered since cement also imported may be more readily available in the
Republic of Palau
The mechanism of cement stabilization is similar to that described above for lime
stabilization One of the more common uses of cement stabilization is for the
improvement of pumping subgrade soils Where a pumping subgrade condition is
encountered during compaction of the subgrade soils very moist saprolite soils
the pumping subgrade may be stabilized by cement treatment to reduce the
potential for pumping subgrade conditions Cement treatment of pumping
subgrade conditions may be applied to subgrade preparation and fill placement As
a preliminary guide one sack of cement 42 kg may be used for approximately
4 square meters of subgrade area This mix should be adjusted in the field based
on the exposed subgrade soil conditions
W O 3595 00 B Page 55
GEOLABS HAWAI
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
2 Ground Stabilization Fabrics
Construction on weak and or wet soils can cause delays and added material costs
In addition these types of soils cause rapid deterioration of the paved structures
resulting in significant maintenance costs during the life of the pavement structure
Construction fabrics have been used in recent years to provide a cost effective
solution where stabilization of the subgrade soils is needed in order to facilitate the
project construction and to enhance the long term performance of the pavement
structure Utilization of ground stabilization fabrics helps to solve these soil related
construction and maintenance problems and keep costs down in three ways
unpaved roads and other areas when designed with ground stabilization
fabrics require less amount of aggregate
e construction schedules are more easily followed because work can continue
during most types of weather and
e ground stabilization fabrics protect the aggregate base course against
subgrade intrusion thus significantly reducing costly maintenance
The benefits resulting from the fabric functions of separation confinement and load
distribution are described below
a Separation
Ground stabilization fabrics with puncture and tear resistant properties act
as a separation barrier between the fine grained soils and load distributing
aggregate fill material As a separator it eliminates the loss of costly
aggregate material into the subgrade and prevents the upward pumping of
silt and other contaminating soil fines into the aggregate
b Confinement
Ground stabilization fabrics provide a high friction surface between the
subgrade and the aggregate layer that helps to keep the aggregate in place
This confining action maintains the thickness and hence the intended
load bearing capacity of the aggregate
C Load Distribution
When placed between the subgrade and the aggregate layer ground
stabilization fabric with its high tensile strength and modulus acts to reduce
localized stress by redistributing traffic loads over a wider area of subgrade
W O 3595 00 B Page 56
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
In general these subgrade stabilization measures are more cost effective for higher
design traffic requiring thicker pavement sections For the lower design traffic it is
unlikely that ground stabilization fabrics would be cost effective to reduce the
thickness of the pavement structural section However ground stabilization fabrics
may be used as a means to facilitate earthwork construction in the wet
environment

Causeway B 235A
Ideally the soft soils encountered within the limits of the proposed roadway
alignment should be removed and replaced with suitable backfill materials prior to new fill
placement and or pavement construction However due to the substantial depth and
lateral extent of the soft soil deposits anticipated in some of these areas complete removal
and replacement of the soft soil deposits would be very costly In addition some
W O 3595 00 B Page 58
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
construction difficulties may be anticipated because the use of heavy equipment may not
be feasible in these areas Therefore we believe that an alternate scheme utilizing soft
ground stabilization techniques may be more appropriate than complete removal and
replacement of the soft soil deposits prior to fill placement and or pavement construction
Complete removal and replacement of the soft soils should be considered where the
thickness of the soft soils is on the order of 1 to 2 meters or less in narrow valleys or
gullies and in areas where substantial fills will be placed on top of the drainage structures
which is further discussed in the Special Earthwork Requirements section of this report
Since the surface soils in the soft soil areas are marshy and wet we believe that it
would not have sufficient strength to support construction equipment Therefore a
working platform or stabilization layer should be established prior to filling of the area
The working platform may consist of a woven geotextile Mirafi 600X or equivalent or
geogrid or equivalent overlain by a layer of surge rock fill The woven
geotextile would eliminate the strict gradation requirements of the working platform since it
would act as a separator to prevent gravel loss into the soft ground and prevent the mud
and fine soil particles from contaminating the surge rock fill It also provides tensile
reinforcement to the surge rock fill and permits earlier use of heavy equipment on a
minimum thickness of surge rock fill A layer of geogrid would be more appropriate when
compared to the woven geotextile in areas where remnants from the clearing operations
may tear the woven geotextile The working platform should extend beyond the toe of the
fill slope for a sufficient distance to allow for construction of the fill embankment
In general the stabilization layer should consist of surge rock fill extending to a
minimum of 0 5 meters above the high water level or at least 0 75 meters in thickness
whichever is greater Where the thickness of the stabilization layer is greater than
1 meter we recommend that a layer of geogrid or equivalent spaced
at 1 meter intervals be incorporated into the stabilization layer for additional
reinforcement A layer of non woven geotextile should be provided between the surge
rock fill and the upper general fill Graphic illustration of the stabilization layer is presented
on Plate 7 Soft Ground Stabilization
W O 3595 00 B Page 59
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
Due to the soft and yielding nature of the subsoils new fills should be placed in thin
level lifts at slow filling rates to reduce the possibility of shear failure in the soft material
It is estimated that the initial portion of the fill placement at the soft soil areas may proceed
at a rate of not more than 1 meter high per week The controlled rate of filling should be
maintained to allow the consolidating soils to gain sufficient strength to support the next lift
The actual filling rate in the soft soil areas should be established in the field based on
settlement gauge and settlement marker readings and adjusted to meet field conditions

F Causeway Fill
A portion of the proposed roadway alignment for Package B will traverse a bay in
the southeastern part of Ngiwal State and will be constructed as a causeway
Therefore we envision that fill placement over water and the soft loose lagoonal
deposits between about and of the Package B alignment will
be required for construction of the proposed Ngiwal Causeway embankment A large
portion of the causeway will be flat with a finished elevation of about 2 2 meters MSL
Based on our field exploration and the plan and profile provided we anticipate fill
W O 3595 00 B Page 62
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
heights to be generally about 2 to 3 meters high with fills up to about 14 meters above
the existing mudline at the abutments for the causeway bridges In general it is our
opinion that the causeway embankment section should consist of the following layers
from top to bottom
Pavement Structure
Granular Fill
A layer of Non Woven Geotextile
Surge Rock Fill up to 0 5 meters above the High Water Level
A layer of Woven Geotextile
Existing Ground or Mudline
We understand that it may be desirable to use clean granular fill to construct the
causeway from the mudline to above the water level in lieu of surge rock fill If granular
fills are planned to be used to fill below the water level the granular fill will need to be
clean not more than 10 percent particles less than and free draining to
allow quick dissipation of pore pressures during the fill placement below the water level
Ifa relatively clean and free draining granular fill is used to fill the portion of the
causeway fill below the water level it is possible to construct the causeway
embankment consisting entirely of granular fill in lieu of the surge rock fill Where a
clean and free draining granular fill is used in lieu of surge rock fill the layer of
non woven geotextile above the surge rock fill may be omitted
We anticipate that the causeway slopes will be protected by graded stone riprap
and armor stone revetment on the landward and seaward sides respectively Considering
that the face of the causeway fill will be protected and the causeway will be constructed of
granular fill a slope inclination of or flatter may be used in design of the causeway
fill slopes
Based on the subsurface conditions anticipated along the causeway alignment we
believe that the toe of the revetment along the seaward side of the causeway should be
designed based on a loose sand and gravel condition Where hard reef rock condition is
encountered in localized areas we believe that a toe trench with a minimum depth of
0 5 meters below the ground surface or mudline may be provided for the stability of the
W O 3595 00 B Page 63
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
new causeway fill embankment against sliding A similar toe trench should also be
provided for the graded stone riprap or articulating block mat along the landward side of
the causeway embankment
Based on the subsoil conditions encountered in our drilled borings along the
causeway alignment it appears that the causeway area is generally underlain by a thin
surficial crust overlying soft loose lagoonal deposits and highly weathered rock formation
at greater depths Based on our engineering analyses settlements of the causeway fill on
the order of about 300 to 700 mm may be anticipated along the causeway alignment It is
estimated that 80 to 90 percent of the primary settlement should occur in about 3 to
6 months after the causeway fill is placed For the bridge abutments settlements on the
order of about 500 to or more have been estimated with 80 to 90 percent of the
primary settlements occurring in about 8 to 12 months
In general placement of the causeway fills should be initiated as early as possible
to allow the existing ground to adjust to the new fill loads A settlement monitoring
program should be implemented to evaluate the magnitude and rate of the estimated
settlements during construction prior to installation of the pavement structural section and
improvements such as the causeway bridge and foundations on the causeway fills If the
estimated duration of settlement for the causeway fills need to be shortened to
accommodate the construction schedule a surcharge program may be considered to
accelerate the consolidation of the underlying soft loose subsoils in order to reduce the
settlement waiting period
Several circulation box culverts are planned within the causeway embankment
Considering that the causeway embankment may settle on the order of about 300 to
700 mm we believe that temporary drainage structures should be installed along the
causeway embankment in the interim during the causeway fill placement and settlement
waiting period for circulation purposes After the rate of settlement of the causeway
embankment has slowed down to an acceptable rate the causeway embankment may
then be excavated to remove the temporary drainage structures and installation of the
permanent circulation box culvert structures may commence
W O 3595 00 B Page 64
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
G Ground Settlement
Ground settlements are anticipated when new fills are placed over the existing
ground to raise the site to the proposed finished elevations These settlements will affect
the construction schedule and the earthwork quantity estimates for the project Based on
the results of our exploration the anticipated ground settlements are primarily the result of
the following two processes
compression of the fill material under its own weight especially since the fill
materials are placed at a lower degree of compaction and
e consolidation of the in situ soils induced by the new fill loads especially
where new fills are placed over soft and or loose mangrove swamp and
lagoonal deposits
1 Settlement of Fill Materials
We have conducted a series of modified consolidation tests to evaluate the
compression characteristics of the saprolite soils when recompacted and used as
fill materials Samples of the saprolite soils were remolded to about 85 percent
relative compaction near the natural moisture content to simulate the anticipated fill
placement conditions during construction The soil samples were subjected to
different surcharge loads to evaluate the settlement behavior of embankment fills
with varying thickness Based on the results of our laboratory tests and
engineering analyses we estimate the following settlements may occur

3 Settlement Waiting Period and Settlement Monitoring
As previously indicated some settlement of the fill material under its own weight
especially in fill areas greater than 3 meters thick should be expected due to the
reduced compaction criteria for fill placement In order to reduce the effects of the
anticipated settlements on the pavement structures and other improvements
constructed on fills a settlement waiting period should be implemented after
placement of the fills and prior to construction of any improvements on the fills The
settlement waiting period may range from a minimum of 1 month to about 5 months
depending on the fill thickness

between the limits of a fill section

It should be recognized that it is difficult to accurately predict the exact time
required for the filled ground to settle since the settlement rates will be affected by
variations of the subsoil structure and the history of the subsoil deposition For the
very soft mangrove swamp and lagoonal deposits with a substantial clay
component we believe that the estimated settlement period could vary by as much
as 50 to 100 percent from the actual settlement period Therefore the actual
settlement rates should be monitored and a settlement monitoring program should
be established to evaluate the magnitude and rate of the estimated settlements
during construction prior to construction of the pavement structure and other
improvements on the fills In addition provisions should be made for potential
delays in the construction schedule if a longer settlement period is required
To monitor the actual settlement rate we recommend that settlement gauges and
settlement markers be installed in areas where fills are placed over soft soils

4 Temporary Surcharge Fills
As previously indicated ground settlements due to fill placement over soft
compressible deposits are anticipated The settlement rates for the soft
compressible deposits would be much slower and would require longer settlement
W O 3595 00 B Page 72
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
waiting periods to reduce the effects of settlement on the improvements
constructed on fills The settlement waiting period for embankment fills constructed
over soft compressible deposits may take months to years depending on the nature
and thickness of the soft compressible deposit
In order to reduce the settlement waiting period and subsequent long term
settlements a temporary surcharge program may be considered for embankment
fill over soft soil areas Surcharge programs can aid in accelerating ground
settlements thus reducing the settlement waiting period Use of temporary
surcharge fills to reduce the settlement waiting period should be left up to the
Contractor as an option However it should be noted that potential slope instability
of the fill embankment may be induced as a result of improperly designed
temporary surcharge fills Therefore details of the temporary surcharge fill program
should be submitted by the Contractor for review and evaluation prior to
implementation
Depending on the fill thickness and the nature of the soft compressible deposit a
30 day waiting period is generally recommended between completion of the
general fill or granular fill and the temporary surcharge fill placement This waiting
period is required to allow dissipation of excess pore pressures in order for the
subsoils to gain strength and to reduce excessive lateral deformation and possible
failure within the soft soils A slope ratio of up to may be used for the
temporary surcharge fill material on top of the fill The surcharge fill may be placed
in lifts of 1 meter high lightly compacted after the end of the 30 day waiting period
Adequate precautions must be taken to reduce erosion of the surcharge fill slopes
during construction and the subsequent surcharge period to attain the required
consolidation The actual settlement waiting period with the temporary surcharge
fill will be determined by settlement gauge or marker readings or vibrating wire
piezometers where appropriate as described in the previous subsection
W O 3595 00 B Page 73
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
H Erosion Control
Implementation of an adequate erosion control program would be crucial to the
satisfactory performance of the planned cut slopes and fill embankments against potential
failures In general erosion of the existing slopes planned cut slopes or new fill
embankments are caused by surface water run off General guidelines such as
establishment of vegetation and provision of interceptor drainage ditches and earth berms
have been provided and discussed under the Cut Slope Design and Fill Slope Design
subsections of this report However we understand that due to the inherent properties of
the on site soils establishment of vegetation at the slope face would be difficult and may
require a long period of time Therefore other erosion control methods or applications
such as Grouted Rubble Paving GRP and concrete lining may need to be considered as
part of the erosion control program of the proposed roadway project In addition to these
conventional rigid lining systems the use of flexible geosynthetic matting as a viable
solution for slope erosion control has become increasingly more acceptable and may be
considered for this project
Flexible geosynthetic matting provides a flexible ground armor that resists erosion
while allowing natural vegetation to establish Its primary and secondary functions include
the following 1 temporary erosion control 2 mulching 3 permanent erosion control
and 4 run off velocity reduction
1 Temporary Erosion Control
The flexibility weight web structure and porosity features of the flexible
geosynthetic matting make it particularly effective in temporary erosion control It
conforms easily to the ground surface and remains firmly in place as a stable
ground armor The mat shields soil surface from the erosive forces of wind and rain
and prevents soil seed and fertilizer from washing away Finally it provides a
rough surface to retain sediment deposits during run off
2 Mulching
The high porosity web structure of the flexible geosynthetic matting acts as a
non deteriorating mulch It holds soil seed and fertilizer in place and retains
W O 3595 00 B Page 74
GEOLABS HAWAH
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
sediment run off as a medium for root growth It also retains moisture and heat
necessary for germination and allows uninhibited growth of grass and other
vegetation
3 Permanent Erosion Control
Once vegetation growth is established the flexible geosynthetic matting continues
to provide permanent erosion control as it becomes embedded in the new stem and
root system It protects new vegetation from washout and reinforces and anchors
the vegetation in place as a cohesive unit
4 Run Off Velocities Reduction
Revegetated slopes and ditches reinforced with the matting reduce run off flow
velocities which is an important advantage that cannot be achieved with concrete
lining It reduces run off flow volume and concentrations and reduces the need for
energy dissipation at the ditch outlet or slope base It also promotes water
percolation and ground water recharge
In order to provide the background soil data and parameters pertaining to the
design and application of the flexible geosynthetic matting and vegetative growth four soil
samples typical of the saprolite soils anticipated at the project site were selected for a
chemical soil analysis Results of the chemical soil analysis and_ preliminary
recommendations by Hawaiian Fertilizer Sales Inc are presented in Appendix C of this
report
l Corrosion Potential
Resistivity is generally recognized as one of the most significant soil characteristics
with regard to the corrosivity of the soil In general the lower the resistivity the greater the
potential for corrosion of the buried metallic structure Conversely the higher the
resistivity the less likely the soil will contribute to corrosion The corrosion evaluation
parameters used for concrete pipes depend on the chlorides and sulfates found in the soil
In general the higher the amount of chlorides and sulfates present in the soil the greater
the potential for corrosion of the buried concrete structures Chemical analyses including
W O 3595 00 B Page 75
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
pH minimum resistivity chloride and sulfate were performed on a selected soil sample
obtained during our field exploration for corrosivity evaluation The results of these tests
are presented on Plate B 80 of Appendix B
J Shoreline Protection
In general we understand that an armor stone revetment over underlayer stone
along the slope face will be used for protection against the erosive forces of wave action at
the bridge abutments and along the seaward side of the causeway embankment For
protection of the causeway fills along the landward side we understand that either a
graded stone riprap or an articulating block mat may be used In general we recommend
that a layer of woven armored erosion control fabric Mirafi 700X or equivalent be placed
underneath the graded stone riprap or articulating block mat on the landward side and
under the underlayer stone on the seaward side of the causeway embankment The fabric
serves to reduce the potential for the underlying fines from being eroded away through the
voids between the graded stones Placement of the woven armored erosion control fabric
below the graded stone riprap and underlayer stone should be in accordance with the
manufacturer s requirements It is also recommended that the riprap and fabric layer be
extended to appropriate depths below the level of scour potential

SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
Due to the potential for long term settlements along the causeway embankment
we recommend that the pavement section for the causeway embankment be limited to the
aggregate base course option only The cement treated base option is not recommended
for use on the causeway embankment Although granular fill is anticipated beneath the
pavement section for the Ngiwal Causeway the subbase course layer has been
maintained for the causeway pavement section Where the roadway subgrade exposes
the dense volcanic breccia or volcanic tuff rock formation we believe that the subbase
course layer may be eliminated from the pavement section
It should be noted that treatment of the shoulder areas adjacent to the asphalt
concrete pavements is critical to the long term performance of the pavement structure
considering the nature of the on site soils Ponding of water adjacent to the edge of the
pavement structural section will allow infiltration and accumulation of subsurface water
which will likely soften the silty and clayey subgrade soils and cause premature failure of
the pavement structure To reduce the potential for damage to the shoulder areas and
the potential for ponding of water we understand that the shoulder areas will consist of
cement treated subbase As a minimum we recommend that the cement treated
subbase be at least 130 mm in thickness
We understand that concrete gutters are also planned for the proposed roadway
construction In general gutters should consist of 100 mm of concrete constructed on
compacted subgrade soils The concrete gutters should be increased to 150 mm thick
in areas traversed by vehicular traffic on a regular basis such as driveways

access roads As a minimum these unpaved roads should consist of at least 200 mm of
subbase course for connecting roads with a roadway gradient of 8 percent or less The
200 mm subbase course should also be provided below the shoulder areas of the
connecting roads It should be noted that there is a potential for washout of the subbase
course depending on the amount of surface water run off along these access roads
Therefore some maintenance of these access roads should be expected

surface The shoulder areas for these steeper portions of the connecting roads should be
treated to reduce the potential for erosion and raveling of the untreated shoulder areas due
to the steeper connecting road gradients Similar to the paved roadway areas we
recommend that the shoulder areas of these connecting roads be treated with 130 mm of
cement treated subbase

N Subgrade Subbase and Base Preparation
In general we recommend that the pavement subgrades including shoulder and
gutter areas be scarified to a depth of about 200 mm moisture conditioned to at least
W O 3595 00 B Page 79
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
2 percent above the optimum moisture and compacted to a minimum of 90 percent
relative compaction prior to placement of the subbase course aggregate base course and
cement treated subbase layers Relative compaction refers to the in place dry density of
soil expressed as a percentage of the maximum dry density of the same soil established in
accordance with ASTM D 1557 91 or AASHTO T 180 test procedures using the moist
preparation method wet to dry Optimum moisture is the water content percentage by
dry weight corresponding to the maximum dry density
Where shrinkage cracks are noted after compaction of the subgrade we
recommend that the soil be prepared again as recommended above Saturation and
subsequent yielding of the exposed subgrade due to inclement weather and poor drainage
may require over excavation of the soft areas and replacement with well compacted
general fill Due to the generally wet conditions at the site we anticipate that the
Contractor may encounter pumping subgrade conditions during the pavement subgrade
preparation Where pumping conditions are encountered during compaction of the
subgrade soils the subgrade soils may be scarified allowed to dry by aeration and
prepared again as recommended above Considering the generally wet environment at
the site the pumping subgrade conditions may be stabilized by cement treatment As a
preliminary guide one sack of cement 42 kg may be used for approximately 4 square
meters of subgrade area This mix should be adjusted in the field based on the exposed
subgrade soil conditions
The subbase course may consist of sand and gravel mixtures including coralline
sands and gravels with a minimum CBR value of 25 corresponds to an R value of about
25 The subbase course should also be free from lumps clay balls and organic and
other deleterious materials In general the subbase course should be well graded from
coarse to fine with no particles larger than 75 mm in largest dimension In addition the
subbase material should contain not more than 15 percent particles less than
Aggregate base course should consist of crushed rock volcanic breccia or basalt
aggregate with a minimum CBR value of 85 corresponds to an R value of about 80
Aggregate base course should be well graded from coarse to fine with no particles larger
W O 3595 00 B Page 80
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
than 50 mm in largest dimension and should contain less than 10 percent particles less
than
Cement treated base should consist of Portland cement stabilized aggregate base
course The aggregate requirements for the cement treated base should conform to the
requirements for aggregate base course The cement treated base should be stabilized
with adequate cement content to obtain a minimum unconfined compressive strength of at
least based on a 7 day curing period
The cement treated subbase recommended for the shoulder areas should consist
of cement stabilized subbase material The cement treated subbase should be stabilized
with adequate cement content to obtain a minimum unconfined compressive strength of at
least based on a 7 day curing period
The aggregate base course and subbase course materials should be
moisture conditioned to above the optimum moisture content and compacted to at least
95 percent relative compaction in accordance with ASTM Test Designation D 1557 91
or AASHTO T 180 Cement treated base and cement treated subbase for shoulders
should be moisture conditioned to near the optimum moisture content and compacted to a
minimum of 95 percent relative compaction in accordance with ASTM Test Designation
D 558 96 The maximum compacted thickness of each layer should not exceed 200 mm
for the cement treated base cement treated subbase aggregate base course and
subbase course CBR and field density tests should be performed on the actual subgrade
soils encountered during construction to confirm the adequacy of the pavement design
O Pavement Drainage System
One of the primary distress mechanisms observed in pavement structures is
pumping due to saturation of the base subbase and or subgrade soils Based on our
experience with projects having similar soil conditions there is a strong tendency for water
to accumulate at the interface between the granular subbase course and the underlying
clayey pavement subgrade soils In addition free water voids in the pavement section
repeated heavy wheel loads and an erodible base can also cause pumping conditions
W O 3595 00 B Page 81
GEOLABS HAWAIH
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
Unless the pavement subgrade soils are properly prepared and adequate drainage is
provided the accumulated water will likely soften the silty and clayey subgrade soils and
cause premature failure of the pavement Therefore paved areas should be sloped and
drainage gradients maintained to carry all surface water off the site Surface water
ponding should not be allowed anywhere on the site during or after construction
In general the primary source of free water in the pavement structure is surface
water infiltration through cracks and joints in the pavement A major source of infiltrated
moisture is the longitudinal pavement shoulder interface Water also enters the pavement
structure from shallow ditches To reduce moisture infiltration into the pavement structure
and underlying subgrade soils all pavement cracks should be sealed Considering the
high rainfall in this area longitudinal edge drains should be considered along alignments
with relatively flat gradients and transverse drains should be provided at low spots along
the roadway alignment and ford crossings
As mentioned earlier pavement damage is generally caused by poor surface and
subsurface drainage conditions along the roadway alignment Therefore we recommend
that longitudinal edge drains along both sides of the roadway alignment be considered
where the roadway gradient is less than 2 percent Where needed a longitudinal edge
drain collector system with outlet pipes should be provided for positive drainage The
outlets must discharge into appropriate drainage outlet structures such as drainage
ditches culverts or catch basins Longitudinal edge drains will not be required for the
causeway pavement section provided that the asphalt concrete pavement and aggregate
base course extend to the outside edge of the shoulder
The typical edge drain should consist of a conventional edge subdrain
The conventional edge subdrain would consist of a perforated pipe with
perforations facing down surrounded by drain rock The invert of the edge drain
perforated pipe should be at least 200 mm below the bottom of the subbase course as
shown on Plate 13 A non woven geotextile such as Mirafi 180N or equivalent should be
placed around the permeable backfill material drain rock to reduce the potential for
migration of fines into the drainage material Care should be exercised when placing the
W O 3595 00 B Page 82
SECTION V DISCUSSION AND EARTHWORK RECOMMENDATIONS
base subbase and or construction of the surface course to avoid damaging the edge
drain collector pipe As an alternative a geocomposite fin subdrain may be used as the
longitudinal edge drain as shown on Plate 13

One of the most common causes of bridge failures stems from scouring of bridge
foundations from flood or other water flow damage We understand that the
stream channel bed and side slopes of the stream channel at the proposed bridge
structures will not be lined with riprap as a counter measure against scour potential
Therefore consideration for the potential for stream channel scour at the abutment
locations will need to be included in the analyses for the bridge foundations The
geotechnical parameters for the scour analyses are further discussed in the
Stream Channel Material for Scour Analyses section of this report

stream scour we recommend that deep foundations consisting of driven piles and drilled
shafts be used for foundation support of the proposed bridge structures In one area
Bridge No 2 one of the abutment locations is underlain by rock formation and may be
supported by a shallow foundation system

B Stream Channel Material for Scour Analysis
As previously indicated the foundation design of the bridge abutments will need to
take into consideration the potential for stream channel scour Scour is the result of
erosive action of flowing water excavating and carrying away material from the bed and
banks of streams channels Total scour over a period of time generally consists of
three components 1 Aggregation and Degradation 2 Contraction Scour and 3 Local
Scour The rates of scour depend on a number of factors such as the shape and
dimensions of a pier or abutment depth of flow velocity of approach flow size and
gradation of stream channel bed material and bed configuration
One of the factors affecting the scour depth is the grain size characteristic of the
stream channel bed material The median diameter of the stream channel bed material
Ds0 in conjunction with the depth of flow and flow velocity is used to calculate fall
velocity of stream channel bed materials in scour depth analysis In order to evaluate the
size and gradation of the stream channel bed material for scour depth analysis at the
bridge abutment locations soil samples from the drilled borings and bulk samples of the
stream channel bed and or bank materials were retrieved for our laboratory tests
W O 3595 00 B Page 87
GEOLABS HAWAI
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
The stream channel bed materials generally range from clayey silts saprolites and
recent alluvium at Bridge and 3 to silty sands and gravel coralline lagoonal
deposits and causeway fills at Bridge Nos 4 and 5 Sieve analyses with hydrometer tests
were performed on the retrieved soil samples to provide grain size distribution of the
materials The results of our laboratory tests are included on Plates B 3 through B 7 of
Appendix B and were provided to the project Civil Engineer for the scour depth analyses
Based on the results of our laboratory tests the median diameter of the stream channel
bed material Dso at the planned bridge structures was determined and presented in the
following table
Scour analyses were performed by the project Civil Engineer for the 100 year storm
event only due to the lack of data for extrapolating to the 500 year storm event Based on

abutments The foundation design recommendations presented in the following sections

SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
C Shallow Foundations
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered in our drilled borings at the major
stream crossings we believe that Abutment 2 north abutment of Bridge No 2 may be
supported on shallow foundations bearing on the very dense volcanic breccia encountered
at a depth of about 1 meter below the existing ground surface The wing walls of the
bridge extending back from the bridge structure may also be supported on shallow
foundations bearing on the dense volcanic breccia The dense volcanic breccia may be
considered a non erodible surface based on a rock classification with a Rock Quality
Designation RQD of more than 50 percent and should not be affected by scour
Based on the Load and Resistance Factor Design LRFD methods for design of
highway bridges we believe that an ultimate bearing pressure of up to 900 kilopascals
kPa may be used to evaluate the extreme event limit state of the footings bearing on the
very dense volcanic breccia encountered at shallow depths The extreme event limit state
of the footings is generally due to unique occurrences such as seismic loading conditions
To evaluate the strength limit state of the foundations a bearing pressure of up to 540 kPa
may be used based on a resistance factor of 0 6
In general footings should be embedded a minimum of 500 mm below the lowest
adjacent finish grade or extended deeper to bear on the dense volcanic breccia formation
In addition where the footings will be affected by scour potential the footings should be
embedded below the scour elevation bear on a non erodible surface or be supported on
a deep foundation system In order for the north abutment footing Bridge No 2 to bear
directly on the dense volcanic breccia as recommended we believe that an excavation on
the order of about 1 to 2 meters below the bottom of footing elevation will be needed to
expose the dense volcanic breccia It should be noted that soil backfill may not be used
below the footing under most circumstances If backfilling of a foundation excavation
exposing the dense volcanic breccia is necessary the backfill material will need to be
concrete to be consistent with a non erodible surface such as the rock
If foundations are located next to utility trenches or easements they should be
embedded below a 1H 1V imaginary plane extending upward from the bottom edge of the
W O 3595 00 B Page 89
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
utility trench or as deep as the inverts of the utility lines This requirement is necessary to
avoid surcharging adjacent below grade structures with additional structural loads and to
reduce the potential for foundation settlement
Based on a service limit state bearing pressure of 300 kPa we estimate that footing
settlements for foundations bearing on the very dense volcanic breccia or concrete as
recommended herein to be less than 25 mm total with differential settlements on the order
of about 15 mm
Lateral loads acting on the bridge structure may be resisted by frictional resistance
between the base of the footings and the bearing material and by passive earth pressure
developed against the near vertical faces of the embedded portion of footings
A coefficient of friction of 0 64 and 0 75 may be used for the shallow footings bearing on
the very dense volcanic breccia or concrete for the strength limit state and extreme event
limit state respectively
Resistance due to passive pressure for the extreme event limit state may be
calculated using an equivalent fluid pressure of 55 kilonewtons per square meter per
meter of depth kKN m for footings embedded in the medium dense in situ sandy soils or
compacted fill materials A reduced passive resistance value of 30 KN m should be used
for footings embedded below the groundwater level if applicable Unless covered by
pavements or slabs the passive resistance in the upper 300 mm should be neglected A
resistance factor of 0 5 may be applied to the passive earth resistances provided above for
evaluating the strength limit state of the foundations
Because of the potential for variation in the depth to the volcanic breccia formation
and the relatively high bearing pressures provided we recommend that an engineer from
Geolabs Hawaii or a qualified geotechnical engineer be present at the site to confirm the
foundation bearing conditions and the required embedment depths prior to the placement
of reinforcing steel or concrete
W O 3595 00 B Page 90
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
D Drilled Shaft Foundations
Our field exploration at the proposed abutment locations of Bridge and 3
except for Abutment 2 of Bridge No 2 generally encountered soft and or loose recent
alluvial deposits overlying stiff saprolite soils The upper soil deposits were underlain by
medium hard to hard volcanic breccia or tuff formation at depths ranging from about 13 to
22 meters below the existing ground surface
It should be noted that the bridge abutments are located above soft soil deposits
Therefore placement of the 5 to 7 meters of abutment fills will induce consolidation of the
soft soil deposits and will require special considerations for design of the abutment fills or
the drilled shaft foundations In order to avoid subjecting the drilled shafts to downdrag
loads due to negative skin friction we recommend that a settlement waiting period be
observed after placement of the abutment fills to allow the estimated settlements to occur
prior to foundation installation The special considerations and requirements for abutment
fill placement are further discussed in the Abutment Fills section of this report
Based on the subsurface conditions encountered and the potential for stream
scour we recommend that drilled shaft foundations with a nominal diameter of 1 5 meters
be used to support the abutments of the bridge structures at these major stream
crossings The drilled shaft foundations would derive support principally from adhesion
between the drilled shaft and the stiff saprolites and the medium hard to hard volcanic
breccia or tuff formation encountered in our borings The contribution from end bearing of
the drilled shafts has been discounted due to the potential for caving in of the upper soft
alluvial soils which would preclude obtaining a very clean bottom for end bearing shafts
during construction
Based on our field exploration engineering analyses and the above assumptions
we recommend that drilled shafts with the following compressive load capacities be used
for the extreme event and strength limit states based on LRFD methods for design of
highway bridges For the strength limit state a resistance factor of 0 5 has been applied to
the extreme event limit state capacities for design of the drilled shaft foundations Drilled
shafts should be spaced a minimum of 3 75 meters center to center to avoid further

3 Foundation Settlements
Settlement of the drilled shaft foundations will result primarily from elastic
compression of the drilled shaft and the subgrade response We estimate that the
total settlement of the drilled shaft supported foundation to be less than
15 millimeters with differential settlements between drilled shafts not exceeding
about one half that amount We believe that these settlements are essentially
elastic and should occur as the loads are applied
4 Construction Considerations
The performance of drilled shafts will depend significantly upon the Contractor s
method of construction and construction procedures As a result we recommend
that a representative of Geolabs Hawaii be present to observe the installation of
drilled shafts during construction In our opinion the following may have a
significant impact on the effectiveness and cost of the drilled shaft foundations
Based on our field exploration some of the proposed abutment locations are
underlain by soft recent alluvial deposits consisting of soft clayey silts and soft peat
extending to depths of about 13 meters below the existing ground surface To
reduce the potential for caving in of the drilled holes temporary casing of the drilled
holes will likely be required during drilled shaft installation Care should be
exercised during removal of the temporary casing to reduce the potential for
necking of the drilled shaft Therefore a minimum 2 meter head of concrete
above the bottom of the casing or adequate concrete head to counter hydrostatic
pressures should be maintained during removal of the casing The anticipated high
groundwater levels may also pose construction difficulties since proper observation
of the sides and bottoms of the drilled shaft may not be possible
W O 3595 00 B Page 95
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
Although dense boulders were not encountered in the borings performed at the
proposed bridge locations during our field exploration there exists the potential for
presence of boulders along the stream alignment Therefore the drilled shaft
contractor will need to have the appropriate equipment and tools to drill through
these obstructions where encountered Appropriate measures will also be needed
to avoid dislodging any boulders into the drilled shaft hole during the drilling and
shaft installation process
In addition the drilled shafts are designed to be embedded into the volcanic breccia
rock formation encountered at depth Therefore coring into the dense volcanic
breccia rock formation will be required The drilled shaft contractor will need to
demonstrate that the proposed drilling equipment and coring tools will be able to
install the drilled shafts to the recommended depths and dimensions by
performance of a trial shaft program
Due to the high structural capacities recommended for the drilled shafts the
sidewalls of the drilled shaft holes will need to be rifled with grooves to provide the
necessary contact and side shaft resistance assumed in our analyses The
grooves should be at least 50 mm deep by 75 mm wide and should run spirally
along the shaft circumference at a pitch of about 300 mm The rifling procedure
may be omitted in the hard rock formation and in the soft recent alluvial deposits
The rifling procedure will be required in the saprolite soils and weathered rock
formation
5 Workmanship
The load carrying capacities of drilled shafts depend to a large extent on the
contact between the drilled shafts and the surrounding soils Therefore proper
construction techniques are important The Contractor should exercise care in
drilling the shaft holes in rifling the sides of the drilled shaft holes and in placing
concrete into the holes
W O 3595 00 B Page 96
GFOI I ARS HAWAIH
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
Drilling by methods utilizing drilling fluids is generally not recommended Because
of the high groundwater table encountered at the subject sites concrete placement
by tremie method is anticipated during construction of the drilled shafts
The concrete should be placed in a suitable manner by displacing the water in an
upward fashion from the bottom of the drilled hole A low shrink concrete mix with
high slump 180 to 230 mm slump range should be used to provide close contact
between the drilled shafts and the surrounding soils The concrete should be
placed in a suitable manner to reduce segregation of the aggregates from the
concrete mix A representative of Geolabs Hawaii should be present to observe
the installation of drilled shafts during construction especially since high capacities
are recommended for these drilled shafts
6 Trial Shaft Program
A trial shaft program is normally required and highly recommended for bridge
projects especially when the quality and common practices of the local drilled shaft
contractors are unknown Considering the diameters and structural load capacities
of the drilled shafts we recommend that a trial shaft program including the
performance of a load test be undertaken to fulfill the following objectives
e To examine the adequacy of the methods and equipment proposed by the
Contractor to install the drilled shafts into the dense volcanic breccia
formation
To confirm or modify the estimated tip elevations of the drilled shafts
e To assess the Contracter s method of rifling the sidewalls of the drilled shaft
holes prior to placing tremie concrete
e To assess the Contractor s method of placing and extracting the temporary
casing required for the drilled shaft
To assess the Contractor s method of tremie concrete placement
To achieve these objectives we recommend that the trial shaft program consist of
drilling a 1 5 meter diameter trial shaft extending to a depth of about 25 meters
below the ground surface at the location of the north abutment of Bridge No 1
Abutment 2 The location of the trial shaft should be near but outside of the
abutment foundations After drilling of the trial shaft the trial shaft should be
W O 3595 00 B Page 97
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
structurally reinforced and instrumented with embedment strain gages for load
testing purposes The embedment strain gages should be placed starting from the
bottom at an elevation of about 2 meters above the tip of the trial shaft and
subsequently at 4 meter intervals as shown on the Drilled Shaft Load Test Detail
Plate 19
Due to the high capacities recommended for the drilled shafts a conventional load
test would not be practical and would be costly to conduct Therefore after
installation of the trial shaft a bi directional axial load test should be conducted on
the reinforced trial shaft using an expandable base load cell Osterberg Load Cell
The expandable base load cell will need to be attached to the bottom of the trial
shaft reinforcing cage prior to lowering the cage in place The drilled shaft load test
should be performed in general accordance with the Quick Load Test Method of
ASTM Test Designation D 1143 The trial shaft should be loaded to failure to
evaluate the ultimate side shear resistance of the trial shaft Installation of the
expandable base load cell installation of the embedment strain gages
performance of the bi directional axial load test and analyses of the load test data
should be performed by a professional experienced in these types of load testing
procedures
We recommend that a representative from Geolabs Hawaii be present during the
trial shaft program to evaluate the Contractor s method of drilled shaft installation
and to evaluate the subsurface materials encountered In addition Geolabs Hawaii
should observe the instrumented load test on the reinforced trial shaft It should be
noted that the drilled shaft design was developed from our analysis using the field
exploration data Therefore observation of the drilled shaft installation operations
by Geolabs Hawaii is a vital part of the foundation design to confirm the design
assumptions

1 Predrilling
Based on the results of our field exploration it appears that the abutments for

weathered volcanic breccia respectively Therefore we do not envision that
predrilling the proposed pile locations will be required prior to pile installation It

should be noted that the causeway fill may have been constructed by placement

through the obstruction If an over sized predrilled hole is encountered during
construction the annular space between the piles and predrilled holes should be
grouted with tremie concrete to provide the recommended lateral load capacity

and by shear along the pile surface and the adjacent soils The recommended

group resistance factor of 0 55 has been applied to the values provided The uplift
load capacities provided include the weight of the pile The structural capacity of
the pile member in tension should be checked by the project Structural Engineer

4 Pile Foundation Settlements
Settlement of the pile foundations will result primarily from elastic compression of
the pile member and the subgrade response We estimate that the total settlement
of the pile supported foundation to be less than 25 mm with differential settlements
between piles not exceeding about one half that amount We believe that these
settlements are essentially elastic and should occur as the loads are applied
D Pile Hammer Selection
The piles should be driven with a hammer capable of delivering a rated energy
between 150 000 and 200 000 newton meters The hammer should be equipped
with an energy control level such that driving within the loose soft soils may be
W O 3595 00 B Page 102
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
controlled at a lower energy to reduce tensile stress development within the piles
which could damage or cause cracking of the piles
Determination of driving equipment to be used for this project should take into
consideration the matching of the pile hammer with the weight of the piles to be
driven This will result in the pile hammer efficiently delivering the required energy
to the top of the pile and also reducing damage to the piles Geolabs Hawaii
should review the pile driving equipment proposed to evaluate if the equipment is
capable of installing the piles to the recommended capacity
The Contractor should forward pile and driving equipment data forms for review
prior to construction for evaluation Geolabs Hawaii should review the submittal
and assess the driveability of piles with the use of the selected hammer A wave
equation analysis should be performed after selection of the pile driving equipment
to assess the driveability of the piles and pile driving criteria Due to possible
variation in the length of the piles unit prices should be obtained during bidding for
add ons shorter piles etc
6 Pile Driving Criteria
For preliminary design of the 600 mm round precast prestressed concrete piles
pile driving may be terminated when the aggregate blow counts are at least
12 blows per 25 mm for the last 500 mm or 18 blows per 25 mm for the last
100 mm The preliminary pile driving criteria should be confirmed and or modified
after selection of the pile driving equipment and performance of a wave equation
analysis In addition the pile driving criteria may be modified during the test pile
driving to accommodate variations of soil conditions in the field
Each pile should be driven continuously without interruption A pile may be rejected
or its design capacity may be reduced when the driving resistance is interrupted for
more than 4 hours and the pile cannot be driven to the required depth During soft
driving such as when the pile tip is within soft loose soils the hammer stroke
or energy should be carefully controlled such that significant tensile stresses do
not develop within the pile
W O 3595 00 B Page 103
GEOLABS HAWAII
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
Because variations in soil strength characteristics are likely to exist in the bearing
soils the supporting capacity of each pile should be confirmed in the field by a
representative from our office during driving It should be noted that the pile design
was developed from our pile analysis using the field exploration data A test pile
program should be carried out to confirm our preliminary pile design and establish
the driving criteria The production pile driving criteria may be revised based on the
test pile program
7 Test Pile Program
We recommend that a test pile program be conducted for the proposed bridge
foundation system All test piles should be instrumented for monitoring by a pile
driving analyzer PDA to observe the stresses developed in the test piles and to
evaluate pile bearing capacity In addition the test pile program should also include
at least one load test at each bridge location Bridge Nos 4 and 5 to fulfill the
following objectives
Examine the driveability of piles with the use of the selected hammer
e Estimate the production pile lengths
Assess the test pile driving criteria based on a dynamic analysis of the pile
using a pile driving analyzer which may be modified for use as the
production pile driving criteria
To achieve these objectives we recommend that the test pile program consist of a
minimum of 3 piles at each abutment location total of 12 test piles for Bridge Nos 4
and 5 The test piles may be incorporated into the actual foundation system upon
the evaluation of the load test results Test piles should be cast at least 3 meters
longer than the estimated pile length to account for potential variation in subsurface
conditions In addition the test pile program should provide flexibility to allow for
addition or relocation of the test piles as may be deemed necessary by our office
to suit the conditions encountered during the test pile driving Restriking of all the
test piles will be required as part of the test pile program
The pile load test should be performed in general accordance with ASTM Test
Designation D 1143 by loading the test pile to the ultimate load capacity for the
SECTION VI DISCUSSION AND STRUCTURE RECOMMENDATIONS
single pile condition We recommend that a representative from Geolabs Hawaii
be present during all pile driving operations to observe the actual driving behavior
and to further evaluate the field performance It should be noted that the pile
design and driving criteria recommended were developed from our pile analysis
using the field exploration data Therefore observation of the pile driving
operations by Geolabs Hawaii is necessary to confirm our design assumptions It
is also recommended that the production pile driving be observed by
Geolabs Hawaii so that driving resistances tip elevations and axial load bearing
capacities may be confirmed
F Abutment Fills
Based on the existing grades and the design finish grades at the bridge abutment
locations we envision that new fills up to about 11 to 14 meters high will be required for
construction of the bridge abutment areas Placement of these substantial fills at the
bridge abutments will induce consolidation of the soft loose recent alluvial and lagoonal
deposits underlying the bridge abutment locations As indicated in Section V of this report
complete removal and replacement of the soft soil deposits may not be practical and or
feasible in these areas where the soft soils are in excess of 2 meters thick Therefore we
believe that the use of stage construction may be required at these bridge abutments
where the new fills exceed about 5 meters in height The abutment locations where stage
construction may be required are shown in the following table for reference Detailed
information and recommendations pertaining to the special earthwork requirements are

In general we believe that placement of fills at these bridge abutments should be

ground to adjust to the new fill loads The estimated settlement waiting period required at

waiting period This will allow the anticipated settlements to occur prior to construction of
the bridge foundations including the test pile program
After completion of the fill placement and observation of the settlement waiting

shaft and driven pile foundations should not be subjected to significant downdrag loads
